</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Mercury</b> is the smallest <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and the closest to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. Its orbit around the Sun takes 87.97 Earth days, the shortest of all the Sun's planets. It is named after the Roman god <i>Mercurius</i> (<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a>), god of commerce, messenger of the gods, and mediator between gods and mortals, corresponding to the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> (Ἑρμῆς). Like <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, Mercury orbits the Sun within <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">Earth's orbit</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Inferior_and_superior_planets" title="Inferior and superior planets">inferior planet</a>, and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apparent_distance" title="Apparent distance">apparent distance</a> from the Sun as viewed from Earth never exceeds 28°. This proximity to the Sun means the planet can only be seen near the western horizon <a href="/wiki/Dusk" title="Dusk">after sunset</a> or the eastern horizon <a href="/wiki/Dawn" title="Dawn">before sunrise</a>, usually in <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>. At this time, it may appear as a bright star-like object, but is more difficult to observe than Venus. From Earth, the planet telescopically displays the complete range of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_phase" title="Planetary phase">phases</a>, similar to Venus and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, which recurs over its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">synodic period</a> of approximately 116 days.
</p><p>Mercury rotates in a way that is unique in the Solar System. It is <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a> with the Sun in a 3:2 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">spin–orbit resonance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> meaning that relative to the <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed stars</a>, it rotates on its axis exactly three times for every two revolutions it makes around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rotation/revolution_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-rotation/revolution-18">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orbit_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-orbit-19">[18]</a></sup> As seen from the Sun, in a <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">frame of reference</a> that rotates with the orbital motion, it appears to rotate only once every two Mercurian years. An observer on Mercury would therefore see only one day every two Mercurian years.
</p><p>Mercury's axis has the smallest <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">tilt</a> of any of the Solar System's planets (about <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">30</span></span> degree). Its <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> is the largest of all known planets in the Solar System;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dwarf_planet_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-dwarf_planet-20">[b]</a></sup> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>, Mercury's distance from the Sun is only about two-thirds (or 66%) of its distance at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a>. Mercury's surface appears heavily cratered and is similar in appearance to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>'s, indicating that it has been geologically inactive for billions of years. Having almost no atmosphere to retain heat, it has surface temperatures that vary diurnally more than on any other planet in the Solar System, ranging from 100 K (−173 °C; −280 °F) at night to 700 K (427 °C; 800 °F) during the day across the equatorial regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[19]</a></sup> The polar regions are constantly below 180 K (−93 °C; −136 °F). The planet has no known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>.
</p><p>Two spacecraft have visited Mercury: <i><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a></span></i> flew by in 1974 and 1975; and <i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i>, launched in 2004, orbited Mercury over 4,000 times in four years before exhausting its fuel and crashing into the planet's surface on April 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EclipseReboost_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-EclipseReboost-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pressurant2015_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pressurant2015-24">[22]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a></i> spacecraft is planned to arrive at Mercury in 2025.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span></h2>
<p>The ancients knew Mercury by different names depending on whether it was an evening star or a morning star. By about 350 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> had realized the two stars were one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-25">[23]</a></sup> They knew the planet as Στίλβων <i>Stilbōn</i>, meaning "twinkling", and Ἑρμής <i><a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermēs</a></i>, for its fleeting motion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> a name that is retained in modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (Ερμής <i>Ermis</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> The Romans named the planet after the swift-footed Roman messenger god, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a> (Latin <i>Mercurius</i>), which they equated with the Greek Hermes, because it moves across the sky faster than any other planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> for Mercury is a stylized version of Hermes' <a href="/wiki/Caduceus" title="Caduceus">caduceus</a>; a Christian cross was added in the 16th century: <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_symbol_(fixed_width).svg" title="☿"><img alt="☿" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/16px-Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/24px-Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/32px-Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones-1999_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones-1999-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<p>Mercury is one of four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, and is a rocky body like Earth. It is the smallest planet in the Solar System, with an <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial</a> <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> of 2,439.7 kilometres (1,516.0 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-3">[3]</a></sup> Mercury is also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_radius" title="List of Solar System objects by radius">smaller</a>—albeit more massive—than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons_by_diameter" title="List of moons by diameter">largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a> in the Solar System, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. Mercury consists of approximately 70% metallic and 30% <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="1599" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg/350px-Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg/525px-Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg/700px-Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_with_magnetic_field.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury's internal structure and magnetic field</div></div></div>
<p>Mercury appears to have a solid silicate crust and mantle overlying a solid, iron sulfide outer core layer, a deeper liquid core layer, and a solid inner core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> The planet's density is the second highest in the Solar System at 5.427 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, only slightly less than Earth's density of 5.515 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-fact-3">[3]</a></sup> If the effect of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_compression" title="Gravitational compression">gravitational compression</a> were to be factored out from both planets, the materials of which Mercury is made would be denser than those of Earth, with an uncompressed density of 5.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup> versus Earth's 4.4 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> Mercury's density can be used to infer details of its inner structure. Although Earth's high density results appreciably from gravitational compression, particularly at the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>, Mercury is much smaller and its inner regions are not as compressed. Therefore, for it to have such a high density, its core must be large and rich in iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The radius of Mercury's core is estimated to be 2,020 ± 30 km (1,255 ± 19 mi), based on interior models constrained to be consistent with the value of the moment of inertia factor given in the infobox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot2012_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Margot2012-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauck_etal_2013_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hauck_etal_2013-36">[34]</a></sup> Hence, Mercury's core occupies about 57% of its volume; for Earth this proportion is 17%. Research published in 2007 suggests that Mercury has a molten core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cornell_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrao_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrao-38">[36]</a></sup> Surrounding the core is a 500–700 km (310–430 mi) <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> consisting of silicates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn2001_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn2001-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Based on data from the <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> mission and Earth-based observation, Mercury's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> is estimated to be 35 km (22 mi) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Padovan2015_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Padovan2015-41">[39]</a></sup> However, this model may be an overestimate and the crust could be 26 ± 11 km (16.2 ± 6.8 mi) thick based on an Airy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isostacy" title="Isostacy">isostacy</a> model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> One distinctive feature of Mercury's surface is the presence of numerous narrow ridges, extending up to several hundred kilometers in length. It is thought that these were formed as Mercury's core and mantle cooled and contracted at a time when the crust had already solidified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury's core has a higher iron content than that of any other major planet in the Solar System, and several theories have been proposed to explain this. The most widely accepted theory is that Mercury originally had a metal–silicate ratio similar to common <a href="/wiki/Chondrite" title="Chondrite">chondrite</a> meteorites, thought to be typical of the Solar System's rocky matter, and a mass approximately 2.25 times its current mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benz_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benz-46">[44]</a></sup> Early in the Solar System's history, Mercury may have been struck by a <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimal</a> of approximately 1/6 Mercury's mass and several thousand kilometers across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benz_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benz-46">[44]</a></sup> The impact would have stripped away much of the original crust and mantle, leaving the core behind as a relatively major component.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benz_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Benz-46">[44]</a></sup> A similar process, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">giant impact hypothesis</a>, has been proposed to explain the formation of the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benz_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-Benz-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, Mercury may have formed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> before the Sun's energy output had stabilized. It would initially have had twice its present mass, but as the <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protosun</a> contracted, temperatures near Mercury could have been between 2,500 and 3,500 K and possibly even as high as 10,000 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CameronAGW1_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-CameronAGW1-47">[45]</a></sup> Much of Mercury's surface rock could have been vaporized at such temperatures, forming an atmosphere of "rock vapor" that could have been carried away by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CameronAGW1_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-CameronAGW1-47">[45]</a></sup> A third hypothesis proposes that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> caused <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a> on the particles from which Mercury was <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accreting</a>, which meant that lighter particles were lost from the accreting material and not gathered by Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each hypothesis predicts a different surface composition, and two space missions have been tasked with making observations of this composition. The first <i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i>, which ended in 2015, found higher-than-expected potassium and sulfur levels on the surface, suggesting that the giant impact hypothesis and vaporization of the crust and mantle did not occur because said potassium and sulfur would have been driven off by the extreme heat of these events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmon20110929_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-csmon20110929-49">[47]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a></i>, which will arrive at Mercury in 2025, will make observations to test these hypotheses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-Bepi_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-Bepi-50">[48]</a></sup> The findings so far would seem to favor the third hypothesis; however, further analysis of the data is needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intra_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-intra-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_geology">Surface geology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mercury" title="Geology of Mercury">Geology of Mercury</a></div>
<p>Mercury's surface is similar in appearance to that of the Moon, showing extensive <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">mare</a>-like plains and heavy cratering, indicating that it has been geologically inactive for billions of years. It is more <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_and_heterogeneity" title="Homogeneity and heterogeneity">heterogeneous</a> than the surface of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> or the Moon, both of which contain significant stretches of similar geology, such as <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">maria</a> and plateaus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awst169_18_18_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-awst169_18_18-52">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">Albedo</a> features are areas of markedly different reflectivity, which include impact craters, the resulting ejecta, and <a href="/wiki/Ray_system" title="Ray system">ray systems</a>. Larger albedo features correspond to higher reflectivity plains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> Mercury has dorsa (also called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrinkle-ridge" title="Wrinkle-ridge">wrinkle-ridges</a>"), Moon-like <a href="/wiki/Highland" title="Highland">highlands</a>, montes (mountains), planitiae (plains), rupes (escarpments), and <a href="/wiki/Vallis_(planetary_geology)" title="Vallis (planetary geology)">valles</a> (valleys).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunneCh7_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunneCh7-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/220px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/330px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/440px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER#Scientific_instruments" title="MESSENGER">MASCS</a> spectrum scan of Mercury's surface by <i>MESSENGER</i></div></div></div>
<p>The planet's mantle is chemically heterogeneous, suggesting the planet went through a <a href="/wiki/Magma_ocean" title="Magma ocean">magma ocean</a> phase early in its history. Crystallization of minerals and convective overturn resulted in layered, chemically heterogeneous crust with large-scale variations in chemical composition observed on the surface. The crust is low in iron but high in sulfur, resulting from the stronger early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_reduction" title="Chemical reduction">chemically reducing</a> conditions than is found in the other terrestrial planets. The surface is dominated by iron-poor pyroxene and olivine, as represented by <a href="/wiki/Enstatite" title="Enstatite">enstatite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forsterite" title="Forsterite">forsterite</a>, respectively, along with sodium-rich <a href="/wiki/Plagioclase" title="Plagioclase">plagioclase</a> and minerals of mixed magnesium, calcium, and iron-sulfide. The less reflective regions of the crust are high in carbon, most likely in the form of graphite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Names for features on Mercury come from a variety of sources. Names coming from people are limited to the deceased. Craters are named for artists, musicians, painters, and authors who have made outstanding or fundamental contributions to their field. Ridges, or dorsa, are named for scientists who have contributed to the study of Mercury. Depressions or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossa_(geology)" title="Fossa (geology)">fossae</a> are named for works of architecture. Montes are named for the word "hot" in a variety of languages. <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">Plains</a> or planitiae are named for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_(god)" title="Mercury (god)">Mercury</a> in various languages. <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">Escarpments</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rup%C4%93s" title="Rupēs">rupēs</a> are named for ships of scientific expeditions. Valleys or valles are named for abandoned cities, towns, or settlements of antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_basins_and_craters">Impact basins and craters</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg/220px-PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg/330px-PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg/440px-PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19421-Mercury-Craters-MunchSanderPoe-20150416.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Enhanced-color image of Munch, Sander and Poe craters amid volcanic plains (orange) near Caloris Basin</div></div></div>
<p>Mercury was heavily bombarded by comets and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> during and shortly following its formation 4.6 billion years ago, as well as during a possibly separate subsequent episode called the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> that ended 3.8 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Mercury received impacts over its entire surface during this period of intense crater formation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunneCh7_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-DunneCh7-55">[53]</a></sup> facilitated by the lack of any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a> to slow impactors down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> During this time Mercury was <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanically</a> active; basins were filled by <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a>, producing smooth plains similar to the maria found on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> One of the most unusual craters is <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_(crater)" title="Apollodorus (crater)">Apollodorus</a>, or "the Spider", which hosts a serious of radiating troughs extending outwards from its impact site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_craters_on_Mercury" title="List of craters on Mercury">Craters on Mercury</a> range in diameter from small bowl-shaped cavities to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-ringed_impact_basin" title="Multi-ringed impact basin">multi-ringed impact basins</a> hundreds of kilometers across. They appear in all states of degradation, from relatively fresh rayed craters to highly degraded crater remnants. Mercurian craters differ subtly from lunar craters in that the area blanketed by their ejecta is much smaller, a consequence of Mercury's stronger surface gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) rules, each new crater must be named after an artist who was famous for more than fifty years, and dead for more than three years, before the date the crater is named.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritzel_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzel-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The largest known crater is <a href="/wiki/Caloris_Planitia" title="Caloris Planitia">Caloris Planitia</a>, or Caloris Basin, with a diameter of 1,550 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientist30012008_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist30012008-65">[63]</a></sup> The impact that created the Caloris Basin was so powerful that it caused <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a> eruptions and left a concentric mountainous ring ~2 km tall surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact crater</a>. The floor of the Caloris Basin is filled by a geologically distinct flat plain, broken up by ridges and fractures in a roughly polygonal pattern. It is not clear whether they are volcanic lava flows induced by the impact or a large sheet of impact melt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Antipodes" title="Antipodes">antipode</a> of the Caloris Basin is a large region of unusual, hilly terrain known as the "Weird Terrain". One hypothesis for its origin is that shock waves generated during the Caloris impact traveled around Mercury, converging at the basin's antipode (180 degrees away). The resulting high stresses fractured the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Alternatively, it has been suggested that this terrain formed as a result of the convergence of ejecta at this basin's antipode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EW1027346412Gnomap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/EW1027346412Gnomap.png/220px-EW1027346412Gnomap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/EW1027346412Gnomap.png/330px-EW1027346412Gnomap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/EW1027346412Gnomap.png/440px-EW1027346412Gnomap.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EW1027346412Gnomap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tolstoj basin is along the bottom of this image of Mercury's limb</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, 46 impact basins have been identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> A notable basin is the 400 km wide, multi-ring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolstoj_Basin" title="Tolstoj Basin">Tolstoj Basin</a> that has an ejecta blanket extending up to 500 km from its rim and a floor that has been filled by smooth plains materials. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven_Basin" title="Beethoven Basin">Beethoven Basin</a> has a similar-sized ejecta blanket and a 625 km diameter rim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup> Like the Moon, the surface of Mercury has likely incurred the effects of <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">space weathering</a> processes, including solar wind and <a href="/wiki/Micrometeorite" title="Micrometeorite">micrometeorite</a> impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plains">Plains</span></h4>
<p>There are two geologically distinct plains regions on Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WagWolIva01_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-WagWolIva01-70">[68]</a></sup> Gently rolling, hilly <a href="/wiki/Inter-crater_plains_on_Mercury" title="Inter-crater plains on Mercury">plains in the regions between craters</a> are Mercury's oldest visible surfaces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup> predating the heavily cratered terrain. These inter-crater plains appear to have obliterated many earlier craters, and show a general paucity of smaller craters below about 30 km in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WagWolIva01_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-WagWolIva01-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smooth plains are widespread flat areas that fill depressions of various sizes and bear a strong resemblance to the lunar maria. Unlike lunar maria, the smooth plains of Mercury have the same albedo as the older inter-crater plains. Despite a lack of unequivocally volcanic characteristics, the localisation and rounded, lobate shape of these plains strongly support volcanic origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup> All the smooth plains of Mercury formed significantly later than the Caloris basin, as evidenced by appreciably smaller crater densities than on the Caloris ejecta blanket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spudis01_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-Spudis01-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compressional_features">Compressional features</span></h4>
<p>One unusual feature of Mercury's surface is the numerous compression folds, or <a href="/wiki/Rupes" title="Rupes">rupes</a>, that crisscross the plains. These also exist on the moon, but are much more prominent on Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> As Mercury's interior cooled, it contracted and its surface began to deform, creating <a href="/wiki/Wrinkle_ridge" title="Wrinkle ridge">wrinkle ridges</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobate_scarp" title="Lobate scarp">lobate scarps</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust faults</a>. The scarps can reach lengths of 1000 km and heights of 3 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi2016.09_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi2016.09-72">[70]</a></sup> These compressional features can be seen on top of other features, such as craters and smooth plains, indicating they are more recent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dzurisin1978_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dzurisin1978-73">[71]</a></sup> Mapping of the features has suggested a total shrinkage of Mercury's radius in the range of ~1 to 7 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watters2016_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watters2016-74">[72]</a></sup> Most activity along the major thrust systems probably ended about 3.6–3.7 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> Small-scale thrust fault scarps have been found, tens of meters in height and with lengths in the range of a few km, that appear to be less than 50 million years old, indicating that compression of the interior and consequent surface geological activity continue to the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi2016.09_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Choi2016.09-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watters2016_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watters2016-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">Volcanism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Picasso_crater.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1207" data-file-width="1213" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Picasso_crater.png/220px-Picasso_crater.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Picasso_crater.png/330px-Picasso_crater.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Picasso_crater.png/440px-Picasso_crater.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Picasso_crater.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Picasso_(crater)" title="Picasso (crater)">Picasso crater</a> — the large arc-shaped pit located on the eastern side of its floor are postulated to have formed when subsurface magma subsided or drained, causing the surface to collapse into the resulting void.</div></div></div>
<p>There is evidence for <a href="/wiki/Pyroclastic_flow" title="Pyroclastic flow">pyroclastic flows</a> on Mercury from low-profile <a href="/wiki/Shield_volcano" title="Shield volcano">shield volcanoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerber_2009_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerber_2009-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volcanism_2011_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volcanism_2011-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-becca_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-becca-78">[76]</a></sup> 51 pyroclastic deposits have been identified,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Groudge_2014_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Groudge_2014-79">[77]</a></sup> where 90% of them are found within impact craters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Groudge_2014_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Groudge_2014-79">[77]</a></sup> A study of the degradation state of the impact craters that host pyroclastic deposits suggests that pyroclastic activity occurred on Mercury over a prolonged interval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Groudge_2014_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Groudge_2014-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>A "rimless depression" inside the southwest rim of the Caloris Basin consists of at least nine overlapping volcanic vents, each individually up to 8 km in diameter. It is thus a "<a href="/wiki/Complex_volcano" title="Complex volcano">compound volcano</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_2014_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothery_2014-80">[78]</a></sup> The vent floors are at least 1 km below their brinks and they bear a closer resemblance to volcanic craters sculpted by explosive eruptions or modified by collapse into void spaces created by magma withdrawal back down into a conduit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_2014_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rothery_2014-80">[78]</a></sup> Scientists could not quantify the age of the volcanic complex system but reported that it could be on the order of a billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_2014_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rothery_2014-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_conditions_and_exosphere">Surface conditions and exosphere</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">Atmosphere of Mercury</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="1899" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg/220px-North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg/330px-North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg/440px-North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_pole_of_Mercury_--_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite of the north pole of Mercury, where NASA confirmed the discovery of a large volume of water ice, in permanently dark craters that are found there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes2012-11-28_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes2012-11-28-81">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The surface temperature of Mercury ranges from 100 to 700 K (−173 to 427 °C; −280 to 800 °F)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[19]</a></sup> at the most extreme places: 0°N, 0°W, or 180°W. It never rises above 180 K at the poles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vasa_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-vasa-13">[13]</a></sup> due to the absence of an atmosphere and a steep temperature gradient between the equator and the poles. The <a href="/wiki/Subsolar_point" title="Subsolar point">subsolar point</a> reaches about 700 K during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> (0°W or 180°W), but only 550 K at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> (90° or 270°W).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup>
On the dark side of the planet, temperatures average 110 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vasa_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-vasa-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup>
The intensity of <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> on Mercury's surface ranges between 4.59 and 10.61 times the <a href="/wiki/Solar_constant" title="Solar constant">solar constant</a> (1,370 W·m<sup>−2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the daylight temperature at the surface of Mercury is generally extremely high, observations strongly suggest that ice (frozen water) exists on Mercury. The floors of deep craters at the poles are never exposed to direct sunlight, and temperatures there remain below 102 K, far lower than the global average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> This creates a <a href="/wiki/Cold_trap_(astronomy)" title="Cold trap (astronomy)">cold trap</a> where ice can accumulate. Water ice strongly reflects <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, and observations by the 70-meter <a href="/wiki/Goldstone_Solar_System_Radar" title="Goldstone Solar System Radar">Goldstone Solar System Radar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Array" title="Very Large Array">VLA</a> in the early 1990s revealed that there are patches of high radar <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflection</a> near the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> Although ice was not the only possible cause of these reflective regions, astronomers think it was the most likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The icy regions are estimated to contain about 10<sup>14</sup>–10<sup>15</sup> kg of ice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle1_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle1-88">[86]</a></sup> and may be covered by a layer of <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a> that inhibits <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> By comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic</a> ice sheet on Earth has a mass of about 4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001180000000000000♠"></span>18</span></sup> kg, and Mars's south polar cap contains about 10<sup>16</sup> kg of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle1_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle1-88">[86]</a></sup> The origin of the ice on Mercury is not yet known, but the two most likely sources are from <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> of water from the planet's interior or deposition by impacts of comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle1_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle1-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury is too small and hot for its <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> to retain any significant <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> over long periods of time; it does have a tenuous surface-bounded <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> containing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milillo_et_al_2005_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Milillo_et_al_2005-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berezhnoy2018_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berezhnoy2018-16">[16]</a></sup> at a surface pressure of less than approximately 0.5 nPa (0.005 picobars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-fact-3">[3]</a></sup> This exosphere is not stable—atoms are continuously lost and replenished from a variety of sources. <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">Hydrogen atoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium_atom" title="Helium atom">helium atoms</a> probably come from the solar wind, <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusing</a> into Mercury's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> before later escaping back into space. <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">Radioactive decay</a> of elements within Mercury's crust is another source of helium, as well as sodium and potassium. <i>MESSENGER</i> found high proportions of calcium, helium, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, oxygen, potassium, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and sodium. Water vapor is present, released by a combination of processes such as: comets striking its surface, <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a> creating water out of hydrogen from the solar wind and oxygen from rock, and sublimation from reservoirs of water ice in the permanently shadowed polar craters. The detection of high amounts of water-related ions like O<sup>+</sup>, OH<sup><span style="color:black;">−</span></sup>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup></a> was a surprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> Because of the quantities of these ions that were detected in Mercury's space environment, scientists surmise that these molecules were blasted from the surface or exosphere by the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sodium, potassium and calcium were discovered in the atmosphere during the 1980–1990s, and are thought to result primarily from the vaporization of surface rock struck by micrometeorite impacts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killen2007_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Killen2007-95">[93]</a></sup> including presently from <a href="/wiki/Comet_Encke" title="Comet Encke">Comet Encke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> In 2008, magnesium was discovered by <i>MESSENGER</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClintock2009_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-McClintock2009-97">[95]</a></sup> Studies indicate that, at times, sodium emissions are localized at points that correspond to the planet's magnetic poles. This would indicate an interaction between the magnetosphere and the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chaikin1_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin1-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>On November 29, 2012, NASA confirmed that images from <i>MESSENGER</i> had detected that craters at the north pole contained <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a>. <i>MESSENGER</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> principal investigator <a href="/wiki/Sean_Solomon" title="Sean Solomon">Sean Solomon</a> is quoted in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> estimating the volume of the ice to be large enough to "encase Washington, D.C., in a frozen block two and a half miles deep".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes2012-11-28_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes2012-11-28-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to NASA, Mercury is not a suitable planet for Earth-like life. It has a <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">surface boundary exosphere</a> instead of a layered atmosphere, extreme temperatures, and high solar radiation. It is unlikely that any living beings can withstand those conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Some parts of the subsurface of Mercury may have been <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a>, and perhaps <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_form" title="Life form">life forms</a>, albeit likely primitive <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, may have existed on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20200324_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20200324-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SR-20200316_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-SR-20200316-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_and_magnetosphere">Magnetic field and magnetosphere</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_magnetic_field" title="Mercury's magnetic field">Mercury's magnetic field</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="2432" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg/220px-Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg/330px-Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg/440px-Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Magnetic_Field_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph showing relative strength of Mercury's magnetic field</div></div></div>
<p>Despite its small size and slow 59-day-long rotation, Mercury has a significant, and apparently global, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. According to measurements taken by <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span>, it is about 1.1% the strength of Earth's. The magnetic-field strength at Mercury's equator is about <span class="nowrap">300 <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">nT</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> Like that of Earth, Mercury's magnetic field is <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipolar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chaikin1_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin1-98">[96]</a></sup> Unlike Earth's, Mercury's poles are nearly aligned with the planet's spin axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qq_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-qq-105">[103]</a></sup> Measurements from both the <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> and <i>MESSENGER</i> space probes have indicated that the strength and shape of the magnetic field are stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qq_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-qq-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is likely that this magnetic field is generated by a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">dynamo</a> effect, in a manner similar to the magnetic field of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> This dynamo effect would result from the circulation of the planet's iron-rich liquid core. Particularly strong <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a> effects caused by the planet's high orbital eccentricity would serve to keep part of the core in the liquid state necessary for this dynamo effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn2001_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn2001-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury's magnetic field is strong enough to deflect the solar wind around the planet, creating a magnetosphere. The planet's magnetosphere, though small enough to fit within Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chaikin1_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin1-98">[96]</a></sup> is strong enough to trap solar wind <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>. This contributes to the space weathering of the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qq_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-qq-105">[103]</a></sup> Observations taken by the <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> spacecraft detected this low energy plasma in the magnetosphere of the planet's nightside. Bursts of energetic particles in the planet's magnetotail indicate a dynamic quality to the planet's magnetosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chaikin1_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin1-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>During its second flyby of the planet on October 6, 2008, <i>MESSENGER</i> discovered that Mercury's magnetic field can be extremely "leaky". The spacecraft encountered magnetic "tornadoes" – twisted bundles of magnetic fields connecting the planetary magnetic field to interplanetary space – that were up to <span class="nowrap">800 km</span> wide or a third of the radius of the planet. These twisted magnetic flux tubes, technically known as <a href="/wiki/Flux_transfer_event" title="Flux transfer event">flux transfer events</a>, form open windows in the planet's magnetic shield through which the solar wind may enter and directly impact Mercury's surface via <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">magnetic reconnection</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA060209_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA060209-109">[107]</a></sup> This also occurs in Earth's magnetic field. The <i>MESSENGER</i> observations showed the reconnection rate is ten times higher at Mercury, but its proximity to the Sun only accounts for about a third of the reconnection rate observed by <i>MESSENGER</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA060209_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA060209-109">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Orbit.2C_rotation.2C_and_longitude"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit,_rotation,_and_longitude">Orbit, rotation, and longitude</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:192px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg/192px-ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg/288px-ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg/384px-ThePlanets_Orbits_Mercury_PolarView.svg.png 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Orbit of Mercury (2006)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:192px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercuryorbitsolarsystem.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="248" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Mercuryorbitsolarsystem.gif/192px-Mercuryorbitsolarsystem.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ab/Mercuryorbitsolarsystem.gif 1.5x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Animation of Mercury's and Earth's revolution around the Sun</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Mercury has the most <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> orbit of all the planets in the Solar System; its eccentricity is 0.21 with its distance from the Sun ranging from 46,000,000 to 70,000,000 km (29,000,000 to 43,000,000 mi). It takes 87.969 Earth days to complete an orbit. The diagram illustrates the effects of the eccentricity, showing Mercury's orbit overlaid with a circular orbit having the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a>. Mercury's higher velocity when it is near perihelion is clear from the greater distance it covers in each 5-day interval. In the diagram, the varying distance of Mercury to the Sun is represented by the size of the planet, which is inversely proportional to Mercury's distance from the Sun. This varying distance to the Sun leads to Mercury's surface being flexed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_bulge" title="Tidal bulge">tidal bulges</a> raised by the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> that are about 17 times stronger than the Moon's on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> Combined with a 3:2 <a href="#Spin-orbit_resonance">spin–orbit resonance</a> of the planet's rotation around its axis, it also results in complex variations of the surface temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup>
The resonance makes a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_day" title="Solar day">solar day</a> (the length between two <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy)" title="Meridian (astronomy)">meridian</a> transits of the Sun) on Mercury last exactly two Mercury years, or about 176 Earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compare_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-compare-111">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury's orbit is inclined by 7 degrees to the plane of Earth's orbit (the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>), the largest of all eight known solar planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2019_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2019-112">[110]</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury" title="Transit of Mercury">transits of Mercury</a> across the face of the Sun can only occur when the planet is crossing the plane of the ecliptic at the time it lies between Earth and the Sun, which is in May or November. This occurs about every seven years on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury's <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> is almost zero,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmic1_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic1-114">[112]</a></sup> with the best measured value as low as 0.027 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot2007_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margot2007-115">[113]</a></sup> This is significantly smaller than that of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, which has the second smallest axial tilt of all planets at 3.1 degrees. This means that to an observer at Mercury's poles, the center of the Sun never rises more than 2.1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minute_of_arc" title="Minute of arc">arcminutes</a> above the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot2007_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Margot2007-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>At certain points on Mercury's surface, an observer would be able to see the Sun peek up a little more than two-thirds of the way over the horizon, then reverse and set before rising again, all within the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_skies#Mercury" title="Extraterrestrial skies">Mercurian day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angular_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-angular-116">[c]</a></sup> This is because approximately four Earth days before perihelion, Mercury's angular <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital velocity</a> equals its angular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_velocity" title="Rotational velocity">rotational velocity</a> so that the Sun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Improper_motion" title="Improper motion">apparent motion</a> ceases; closer to perihelion, Mercury's angular orbital velocity then exceeds the angular rotational velocity. Thus, to a hypothetical observer on Mercury, the Sun appears to move in a <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde</a> direction. Four Earth days after perihelion, the Sun's normal apparent motion resumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup> A similar effect would have occurred if Mercury had been in synchronous rotation: the alternating gain and loss of rotation over revolution would have caused a libration of 23.65° in longitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the same reason, there are two points on Mercury's equator, 180 degrees apart in <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, at either of which, around perihelion in alternate Mercurian years (once a Mercurian day), the Sun passes overhead, then reverses its apparent motion and passes overhead again, then reverses a second time and passes overhead a third time, taking a total of about 16 Earth-days for this entire process. In the other alternate Mercurian years, the same thing happens at the other of these two points. The amplitude of the retrograde motion is small, so the overall effect is that, for two or three weeks, the Sun is almost stationary overhead, and is at its most brilliant because Mercury is at perihelion, its closest to the Sun. This prolonged exposure to the Sun at its brightest makes these two points the hottest places on Mercury. Maximum temperature occurs when the Sun is at an angle of about 25 degrees past noon due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_temperature_variation#Temperature_lag" title="Diurnal temperature variation">diurnal temperature lag</a>, at 0.4 Mercury days and 0.8 Mercury years past sunrise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> Conversely, there are two other points on the equator, 90 degrees of longitude apart from the first ones, where the Sun passes overhead only when the planet is at aphelion in alternate years, when the apparent motion of the Sun in Mercury's sky is relatively rapid. These points, which are the ones on the equator where the apparent retrograde motion of the Sun happens when it is crossing the horizon as described in the preceding paragraph, receive much less solar heat than the first ones described above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury attains inferior conjunction (nearest approach to Earth) every 116 Earth days on average,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-fact-3">[3]</a></sup> but this interval can range from 105 days to 129 days due to the planet's eccentric orbit. Mercury can come as near as 82,200,000 kilometres (0.549 astronomical units; 51.1 million miles) to Earth, and that is slowly declining: The next approach to within 82,100,000 km (51.0 million miles) is in 2679, and to within 82,000,000 km (51 million miles) in 4487, but it will not be closer to Earth than 80,000,000 km (50 million miles) until 28,622.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> Its period of retrograde motion as seen from Earth can vary from 8 to 15 days on either side of inferior conjunction. This large range arises from the planet's high orbital eccentricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup> Essentially because Mercury is closest to the Sun, when taking an average over time, Mercury is the closest planet to the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoreOrLess_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoreOrLess-121">[118]</a></sup> and—in that measure—it is the closest planet to each of the other planets in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[d]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Longitude_convention">Longitude convention</span></h3>
<p>The longitude convention for Mercury puts the zero of longitude at one of the two hottest points on the surface, as described above. However, when this area was first visited, by <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span>, this zero meridian was in darkness, so it was impossible to select a feature on the surface to define the exact position of the meridian. Therefore, a small crater further west was chosen, called <a href="/wiki/Hun_Kal_(crater)" title="Hun Kal (crater)">Hun Kal</a>, which provides the exact reference point for measuring longitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> The center of Hun Kal defines the 20° west meridian. A 1970 International Astronomical Union resolution suggests that longitudes be measured positively in the westerly direction on Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-128">[124]</a></sup> The two hottest places on the equator are therefore at longitudes 0° W and 180° W, and the coolest points on the equator are at longitudes 90° W and 270° W. However, the <i>MESSENGER</i> project uses an east-positive convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArchinalA'Hearn2010_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArchinalA'Hearn2010-129">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spin-orbit_resonance">Spin-orbit resonance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg/220px-Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg/330px-Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg/440px-Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury%27s_orbital_resonance.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After one orbit, Mercury has rotated 1.5 times, so after two complete orbits the same hemisphere is again illuminated.</div></div></div>
<p>For many years it was thought that Mercury was synchronously <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a> with the Sun, <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotating</a> once for each orbit and always keeping the same face directed towards the Sun, in the same way that the same side of the Moon always faces Earth. Radar observations in 1965 proved that the planet has a 3:2 spin-orbit resonance, rotating three times for every two revolutions around the Sun. The eccentricity of Mercury's orbit makes this resonance stable—at perihelion, when the solar tide is strongest, the Sun is nearly still in Mercury's sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rare 3:2 resonant tidal locking is stabilized by the variance of the tidal force along Mercury's eccentric orbit, acting on a permanent dipole component of Mercury's mass distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colombo_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colombo-131">[127]</a></sup> In a circular orbit there is no such variance, so the only resonance stabilized in such an orbit is at 1:1 (e.g., Earth–Moon), when the tidal force, stretching a body along the "center-body" line, exerts a torque that aligns the body's axis of least inertia (the "longest" axis, and the axis of the aforementioned dipole) to point always at the center. However, with noticeable eccentricity, like that of Mercury's orbit, the tidal force has a maximum at perihelion and therefore stabilizes resonances, like 3:2, ensuring that the planet points its axis of least inertia roughly at the Sun when passing through perihelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colombo_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Colombo-131">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original reason astronomers thought it was synchronously locked was that, whenever Mercury was best placed for observation, it was always nearly at the same point in its 3:2 resonance, hence showing the same face. This is because, coincidentally, Mercury's rotation period is almost exactly half of its synodic period with respect to Earth. Due to Mercury's 3:2 spin-orbit resonance, a solar day lasts about 176 Earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal day</a> (the period of rotation) lasts about 58.7 Earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Simulations indicate that the orbital eccentricity of Mercury varies <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotically</a> from nearly zero (circular) to more than 0.45 over millions of years due to <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbations</a> from the other planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Correia2009_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Correia2009-132">[128]</a></sup> 
This was thought to explain Mercury's 3:2 spin-orbit resonance (rather than the more usual 1:1), because this state is more likely to arise during a period of high eccentricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Correia_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Correia-133">[129]</a></sup> 
However, accurate modeling based on a realistic model of tidal response has demonstrated that Mercury was captured into the 3:2 spin-orbit state at a very early stage of its history, within 20 (more likely, 10) million years after its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerical simulations show that a future <a href="/wiki/Secular_resonance" title="Secular resonance">secular</a> <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonant</a> perihelion interaction with Jupiter may cause the eccentricity of Mercury's orbit to increase to the point where there is a 1% chance that the planet will collide with Venus within the next five billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laskar2008_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laskar2008-135">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laskar2009_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laskar2009-136">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_of_perihelion">Advance of perihelion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_precession_of_Mercury" title="Perihelion precession of Mercury">Perihelion precession of Mercury</a></div>
<p>In 1859, the French mathematician and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a> reported that the slow <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> of Mercury's orbit around the Sun could not be completely explained by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_mechanics" title="Newtonian mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a> and perturbations by the known planets. He suggested, among possible explanations, that another planet (or perhaps instead a series of smaller "corpuscules") might exist in an orbit even closer to the Sun than that of Mercury, to account for this perturbation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup> (Other explanations considered included a slight oblateness of the Sun.) The success of the search for <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> based on its perturbations of the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> led astronomers to place faith in this possible explanation, and the hypothetical planet was named <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(hypothetical_planet)" title="Vulcan (hypothetical planet)">Vulcan</a>, but no such planet was ever found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_precession" title="Perihelion precession">perihelion precession</a> of Mercury is 5,600 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcseconds" title="Arcseconds">arcseconds</a> (1.5556°) per century relative to Earth, or 574.10±0.65 arcseconds per century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clemence_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clemence-139">[135]</a></sup> relative to the inertial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Celestial_Reference_Frame" title="International Celestial Reference Frame">ICRF</a>. Newtonian mechanics, taking into account all the effects from the other planets, predicts a precession of 5,557 arcseconds (1.5436°) per century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clemence_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clemence-139">[135]</a></sup> In the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a> provided the explanation for the observed precession, by formalizing gravitation as being mediated by the curvature of spacetime. The effect is small: just 42.98 arcseconds per century for Mercury; it therefore requires a little over twelve million orbits for a full excess turn. Similar, but much smaller, effects exist for other Solar System bodies: 8.62 arcseconds per century for Venus, 3.84 for Earth, 1.35 for Mars, and 10.05 for <a href="/wiki/1566_Icarus" title="1566 Icarus">1566 Icarus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="2158" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mercury.jpg/220px-Mercury.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mercury.jpg/330px-Mercury.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mercury.jpg/440px-Mercury.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image mosaic by <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span>, 1974</div></div></div>
<p>Mercury's <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> is calculated to vary between −2.48 (brighter than <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>) around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superior_conjunction" title="Superior conjunction">superior conjunction</a> and +7.25 (below the limit of naked-eye visibility) around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_conjunction" title="Inferior conjunction">inferior conjunction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-14">[14]</a></sup> The mean apparent magnitude is 0.23 while the standard deviation of 1.78 is the largest of any planet. The mean apparent magnitude at superior conjunction is −1.89 while that at inferior conjunction is +5.93.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-14">[14]</a></sup> Observation of Mercury is complicated by its proximity to the Sun, as it is lost in the Sun's glare for much of the time. Mercury can be observed for only a brief period during either morning or evening twilight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Menzel1964P292-293_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Menzel1964P292-293-142">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>But in some cases Mercury can better be observed in daylight with a telescope when the position is known because it is higher in the sky and less atmospheric effects affect the view of the planet. When proper safety precautions are taken to prevent inadvertently pointing the telescope at the Sun (and thus blinding the user), Mercury can be viewed as close as 4° to the Sun when near superior conjunction when it is almost at its brightest.
</p><p>Mercury can, like several other planets and the brightest stars, be seen during a total <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eclipse_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-eclipse-143">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like the Moon and Venus, Mercury exhibits <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">phases</a> as seen from Earth. It is "new" at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)#Superior_and_inferior" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">inferior conjunction</a> and "full" at superior conjunction. The planet is rendered invisible from Earth on both of these occasions because of its being obscured by the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Menzel1964P292-293_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Menzel1964P292-293-142">[138]</a></sup> except its new phase during a transit.
</p><p>Mercury is technically brightest as seen from Earth when it is at a full phase. Although Mercury is farthest from Earth when it is full, the greater illuminated area that is visible and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_effect" title="Opposition effect">opposition brightness surge</a> more than compensates for the distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MallamaSky_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-MallamaSky-144">[140]</a></sup> The opposite is true for Venus, which appears brightest when it is a <a href="/wiki/Crescent" title="Crescent">crescent</a>, because it is much closer to Earth than when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gibbous" title="Gibbous">gibbous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MallamaSky_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-MallamaSky-144">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1550" data-file-width="2044" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg/220px-PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg/330px-PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg/440px-PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color map showing the maximum temperatures of the north polar region</div></div></div>
<p>Nonetheless, the brightest (full phase) appearance of Mercury is an essentially impossible time for practical observation, because of the extreme proximity of the Sun. Mercury is best observed at the first and last quarter, although they are phases of lesser brightness. The first and last quarter phases occur at greatest <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongation</a> east and west of the Sun, respectively.
