At both of these times Mercury's separation from the Sun ranges anywhere from 17.9° at perihelion to 27.8° at aphelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elongation_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-elongation-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MercHorizons_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-MercHorizons-147">[143]</a></sup> At greatest <i>western</i> elongation, Mercury rises at its earliest before sunrise, and at greatest <i>eastern</i> elongation, it sets at its latest after sunset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RASC2007_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-RASC2007-148">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="1664" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg/220px-PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg/330px-PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg/440px-PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19422-Mercury-CarnegieRupes-MDIS-MLA-20150416.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color image of Carnegie Rupes, a tectonic landform—high terrain (red); low (blue).</div></div></div>
<p>Mercury is more often and easily visible from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> than from the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern</a>. This is because Mercury's maximum western elongation occurs only during early autumn in the Southern Hemisphere, whereas its greatest eastern elongation happens only during late winter in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RASC2007_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-RASC2007-148">[144]</a></sup> In both of these cases, the angle at which the planet's orbit intersects the horizon is maximized, allowing it to rise several hours before sunrise in the former instance and not set until several hours after sundown in the latter from southern mid-latitudes, such as Argentina and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RASC2007_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-RASC2007-148">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternate method for viewing Mercury involves observing the planet during daylight hours when conditions are clear, ideally when it is at its greatest elongation. This allows the planet to be found easily, even when using telescopes with 8 cm (3.1 in) apertures. However, great care must be taken to obstruct the Sun from sight because of the extreme risk for eye damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[145]</a></sup> This method bypasses the limitation of twilight observing when the ecliptic is located at a low elevation (e.g. on autumn evenings).
</p><p>Ground-based telescope observations of Mercury reveal only an illuminated partial disk with limited detail. The first of two <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> to visit the planet was <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span>, which mapped about 45% of its surface from 1974 to 1975. The second is the <i>MESSENGER</i> spacecraft, which after three Mercury flybys between 2008 and 2009, attained orbit around Mercury on March 17, 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insertion_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-insertion-150">[146]</a></sup> to study and map the rest of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsMarX31_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-twsMarX31-151">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> cannot observe Mercury at all, due to safety procedures that prevent its pointing too close to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the shift of 0.15 revolutions in a year makes up a seven-year cycle (0.15 × 7 ≈ 1.0), in the seventh year Mercury follows almost exactly (earlier by 7 days) the sequence of phenomena it showed seven years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elongation_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-elongation-146">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_history">Observation history</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_astronomers">Ancient astronomers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-bonatti.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="566" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mercury-bonatti.png/220px-Mercury-bonatti.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mercury-bonatti.png/330px-Mercury-bonatti.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mercury-bonatti.png/440px-Mercury-bonatti.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-bonatti.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury, from <i>Liber astronomiae</i>, 1550</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known recorded observations of Mercury are from the <a href="/wiki/MUL.APIN" title="MUL.APIN">MUL.APIN</a> tablets. These observations were most likely made by an <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> astronomer around the 14th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform</a> name used to designate Mercury on the MUL.APIN tablets is transcribed as UDU.IDIM.GU\U<sub>4</sub>.UD ("the jumping planet").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuneiform_MUL_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuneiform_MUL-154">[e]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian records</a> of Mercury date back to the 1st millennium BC. The <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> called the planet <a href="/wiki/Nabu" title="Nabu">Nabu</a> after the messenger to the gods in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_mythology" title="Babylonian mythology">their mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHU_history_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHU_history-156">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Egypt" title="Greeks in Egypt">Greco</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heath1921_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heath1921-157">[152]</a></sup> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemaeus" title="Claudius Ptolemaeus">Ptolemy</a> wrote about the possibility of planetary transits across the face of the Sun in his work <i>Planetary Hypotheses</i>. He suggested that no transits had been observed either because planets such as Mercury were too small to see, or because the transits were too infrequent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shatir500.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="473" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Shatir500.jpg/220px-Shatir500.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Shatir500.jpg/330px-Shatir500.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Shatir500.jpg/440px-Shatir500.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shatir500.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Shatir" title="Ibn al-Shatir">Ibn al-Shatir</a>'s model for the appearances of Mercury, showing the multiplication of <a href="/wiki/Deferent_and_epicycle" title="Deferent and epicycle">epicycles</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a>, thus eliminating the Ptolemaic eccentrics and <a href="/wiki/Equant" title="Equant">equant</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, Mercury was known as "the Hour Star" (<i>Chen-xing</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">辰星</span></span>). It was associated with the direction north and the phase of water in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Phases" title="Five Phases">Five Phases</a> system of metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[154]</a></sup> Modern <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_culture" title="Korean culture">Korean</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_culture" title="Japanese culture">Japanese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vietnamese_culture" title="Vietnamese culture">Vietnamese</a> cultures refer to the planet literally as the "water star" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">水星</span></span>), based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">Five elements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a> used the name <a href="/wiki/Budha" title="Budha">Budha</a> for Mercury, and this god was thought to preside over Wednesday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> The god <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> (or Woden) of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a> was associated with the planet Mercury and Wednesday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a> may have represented Mercury as an owl (or possibly four owls; two for the morning aspect and two for the evening) that served as a messenger to the <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">medieval Islamic astronomy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ish%C4%81q_Ibr%C4%81h%C4%ABm_al-Zarq%C4%81l%C4%AB" title="Abū Ishāq Ibrāhīm al-Zarqālī">Abū Ishāq Ibrāhīm al-Zarqālī</a> in the 11th century described the deferent of Mercury's geocentric orbit as being oval, like an egg or a <a href="/wiki/Pine_nut" title="Pine nut">pignon</a>, although this insight did not influence his astronomical theory or his astronomical calculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup> In the 12th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> observed "two planets as black spots on the face of the Sun", which was later suggested as the transit of Mercury and/or Venus by the <a href="/wiki/Maragheh_observatory" title="Maragheh observatory">Maragha</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qotb_al-Din_Shirazi" title="Qotb al-Din Shirazi">Qotb al-Din Shirazi</a> in the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup> (Note that most such medieval reports of transits were later taken as observations of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup>)
</p><p>In India, the <a href="/wiki/Kerala_school_of_astronomy_and_mathematics" title="Kerala school of astronomy and mathematics">Kerala school</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nilakantha_Somayaji" title="Nilakantha Somayaji">Nilakantha Somayaji</a> in the 15th century developed a partially heliocentric planetary model in which Mercury orbits the Sun, which in turn orbits Earth, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic system</a> later proposed by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> in the late 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground-based_telescopic_research">Ground-based telescopic research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transit_Of_Mercury,_May_9th,_2016.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2990" data-file-width="2990" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png/220px-Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png/330px-Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png/440px-Transit_Of_Mercury%2C_May_9th%2C_2016.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transit_Of_Mercury,_May_9th,_2016.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transit of Mercury. Mercury is visible as a black dot below and to the left of center. The dark area above the center of the solar disk is a <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspot</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Planet_Elongation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="223" data-file-width="414" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Planet_Elongation.jpg/220px-Planet_Elongation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Planet_Elongation.jpg/330px-Planet_Elongation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Planet_Elongation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Planet_Elongation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">Elongation</a> is the angle between the Sun and the planet, with Earth as the reference point. Mercury appears close to the Sun.</div></div></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopic</a> observations of Mercury were made by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> from 1610. In 1612, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> observed the brightness of Mercury varied with the planet's orbital position and concluded it had phases "in the same way as Venus and the Moon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup> In 1631, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gassendi" title="Pierre Gassendi">Pierre Gassendi</a> made the first telescopic observations of the transit of a planet across the Sun when he saw a transit of Mercury predicted by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>. In 1639, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Zupi" title="Giovanni Battista Zupi">Giovanni Zupi</a> used a telescope to discover that the planet had <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbital</a> phases similar to Venus and the Moon. The observation demonstrated conclusively that Mercury orbited around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strom_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-strom-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>A rare event in astronomy is the passage of one planet in front of another (<a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a>), as seen from Earth. Mercury and Venus occult each other every few centuries, and the event of May 28, 1737 is the only one historically observed, having been seen by <a href="/wiki/John_Bevis" title="John Bevis">John Bevis</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory" title="Royal Greenwich Observatory">Royal Greenwich Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup> The next occultation of Mercury by Venus will be on December 3, 2133.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difficulties inherent in observing Mercury mean that it was far less studied than the other planets. In 1800, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Schr%C3%B6ter" title="Johann Schröter">Johann Schröter</a> made observations of surface features, claiming to have observed 20-kilometre-high (12 mi) mountains. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel" title="Friedrich Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a> used Schröter's drawings to erroneously estimate the rotation period as 24 hours and an axial tilt of 70°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sao188r_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-sao188r-174">[169]</a></sup> In the 1880s, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Schiaparelli" title="Giovanni Schiaparelli">Giovanni Schiaparelli</a> mapped the planet more accurately, and suggested that Mercury's rotational period was 88 days, the same as its orbital period due to tidal locking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[170]</a></sup> This phenomenon is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">synchronous rotation</a>. The effort to map the surface of Mercury was continued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugenios_Antoniadi" title="Eugenios Antoniadi">Eugenios Antoniadi</a>, who published a book in 1934 that included both maps and his own observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chaikin1_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin1-98">[96]</a></sup> Many of the planet's surface features, particularly the <a href="/wiki/List_of_albedo_features_on_Mercury" title="List of albedo features on Mercury">albedo features</a>, take their names from Antoniadi's map.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1962, Soviet scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Radio-engineering_and_Electronics" title="Institute of Radio-engineering and Electronics">Institute of Radio-engineering and Electronics</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR_Academy_of_Sciences" title="USSR Academy of Sciences">USSR Academy of Sciences</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Kotelnikov" title="Vladimir Kotelnikov">Vladimir Kotelnikov</a>, became the first to bounce a radar signal off Mercury and receive it, starting radar observations of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[174]</a></sup> Three years later, radar observations by Americans <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gordon_H._Pettengill" title="Gordon H. Pettengill">Gordon H. Pettengill</a> and Rolf B. Dyce, using the 300-meter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arecibo_radio_telescope" title="Arecibo radio telescope">Arecibo radio telescope</a> in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, showed conclusively that the planet's rotational period was about 59 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[176]</a></sup> The theory that Mercury's rotation was synchronous had become widely held, and it was a surprise to astronomers when these radio observations were announced. If Mercury were tidally locked, its dark face would be extremely cold, but measurements of radio emission revealed that it was much hotter than expected. Astronomers were reluctant to drop the synchronous rotation theory and proposed alternative mechanisms such as powerful heat-distributing winds to explain the observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2179" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/220px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/330px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/440px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water ice (yellow) at Mercury's north polar region</div></div></div>
<p>Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Colombo" title="Giuseppe Colombo">Giuseppe Colombo</a> noted that the rotation value was about two-thirds of Mercury's orbital period, and proposed that the planet's orbital and rotational periods were locked into a 3:2 rather than a 1:1 resonance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup> Data from <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> subsequently confirmed this view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[179]</a></sup> This means that Schiaparelli's and Antoniadi's maps were not "wrong". Instead, the astronomers saw the same features during every <i>second</i> orbit and recorded them, but disregarded those seen in the meantime, when Mercury's other face was toward the Sun, because the orbital geometry meant that these observations were made under poor viewing conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sao188r_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-sao188r-174">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ground-based optical observations did not shed much further light on Mercury, but radio astronomers using interferometry at microwave wavelengths, a technique that enables removal of the solar radiation, were able to discern physical and chemical characteristics of the subsurface layers to a depth of several meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup> Not until the first space probe flew past Mercury did many of its most fundamental morphological properties become known. Moreover, recent technological advances have led to improved ground-based observations. In 2000, high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Lucky_imaging" title="Lucky imaging">lucky imaging</a> observations were conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a> 1.5 meter Hale telescope. They provided the first views that resolved surface features on the parts of Mercury that were not imaged in the <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup> Most of the planet has been mapped by the Arecibo radar telescope, with 5 km (3.1 mi) resolution, including polar deposits in shadowed craters of what may be water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harm06_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harm06-188">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_with_space_probes">Research with space probes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mercury" title="Exploration of Mercury">Exploration of Mercury</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg/220px-MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg/330px-MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg/440px-MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>MESSENGER</i> being prepared for launch</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif/220px-PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif/330px-PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif/440px-PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18389-MarsCuriosityRover-MercuryTransitsSun-20140603.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury transiting the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> as viewed by the Mars rover <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i></a> (June 3, 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140610_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140610-189">[184]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Reaching Mercury from Earth poses significant technical challenges, because it orbits so much closer to the Sun than Earth. A Mercury-bound spacecraft launched from Earth must travel over 91 million kilometres (57 million miles) into the Sun's gravitational <a href="/wiki/Potential_well" title="Potential well">potential well</a>. Mercury has an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> of 47.4 km/s (29.5 mi/s), whereas Earth's orbital speed is 29.8 km/s (18.5 mi/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2019_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2019-112">[110]</a></sup> Therefore, the spacecraft must make a large change in <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>) to get to Mercury and then enter orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[185]</a></sup> as compared to the delta-v required for, say, <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars" title="Exploration of Mars">Mars planetary missions</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> liberated by moving down the Sun's potential well becomes <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, requiring a delta-v change to do anything other than pass by Mercury. Some portion of this <a href="/wiki/Delta-v_budget" title="Delta-v budget">delta-v budget</a> can be provided from a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> during one or more fly-bys of Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup> To land safely or enter a stable orbit the spacecraft would rely entirely on rocket motors. <a href="/wiki/Aerobraking" title="Aerobraking">Aerobraking</a> is ruled out because Mercury has a negligible atmosphere. A trip to Mercury requires more rocket fuel than that required to <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape</a> the Solar System completely. As a result, only three space probes have visited it so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLprofile1_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPLprofile1-192">[187]</a></sup> A proposed alternative approach would use a <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">solar sail</a> to attain a Mercury-synchronous orbit around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mariner_10"><i>Mariner 10</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2208" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mariner_10.jpg/220px-Mariner_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mariner_10.jpg/330px-Mariner_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mariner_10.jpg/440px-Mariner_10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Mariner 10</i>, the first probe to visit Mercury</div></div></div>
<p>The first spacecraft to visit Mercury was <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> (1974–1975).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-25">[23]</a></sup> The spacecraft used the gravity of Venus to adjust its orbital velocity so that it could approach Mercury, making it both the first spacecraft to use this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">gravitational "slingshot"</a> effect and the first NASA mission to visit multiple planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunneCh4_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunneCh4-194">[189]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> provided the first close-up images of Mercury's surface, which immediately showed its heavily cratered nature, and revealed many other types of geological features, such as the giant scarps that were later ascribed to the effect of the planet shrinking slightly as its iron core cools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[190]</a></sup> Unfortunately, the same face of the planet was lit at each of <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span></span> close approaches. This made close observation of both sides of the planet impossible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[191]</a></sup> and resulted in the mapping of less than 45% of the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USATMessenger_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-USATMessenger-197">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spacecraft made three close approaches to Mercury, the closest of which took it to within 327 km (203 mi) of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtlasM10_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-AtlasM10-198">[193]</a></sup> At the first close approach, instruments detected a magnetic field, to the great surprise of planetary geologists—Mercury's rotation was expected to be much too slow to generate a significant <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamo</a> effect. The second close approach was primarily used for imaging, but at the third approach, extensive magnetic data were obtained. The data revealed that the planet's magnetic field is much like Earth's, which deflects the solar wind around the planet. For many years after the <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> encounters, the origin of Mercury's magnetic field remained the subject of several competing theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness1_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ness1-199">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 24, 1975, just eight days after its final close approach, <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> ran out of fuel. Because its orbit could no longer be accurately controlled, mission controllers instructed the probe to shut down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunneCh8_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-DunneCh8-201">[196]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> is thought to be still orbiting the Sun, passing close to Mercury every few months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="MESSENGER"><i>MESSENGER</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1262" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg/220px-Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg/330px-Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg/440px-Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated details of the impact of <i>MESSENGER</i> on April 30, 2015</div></div></div>
<p>A second NASA mission to Mercury, named <i>MESSENGER</i> (MErcury Surface, Space ENvironment, GEochemistry, and Ranging), was launched on August 3, 2004. It made a fly-by of Earth in August 2005, and of Venus in October 2006 and June 2007 to place it onto the correct trajectory to reach an orbit around Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup> A first fly-by of Mercury occurred on January 14, 2008, a second on October 6, 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MessCountdown_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-MessCountdown-204">[199]</a></sup> and a third on September 29, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[200]</a></sup> Most of the hemisphere not imaged by <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span> was mapped during these fly-bys. The probe successfully entered an elliptical orbit around the planet on March 18, 2011. The first orbital image of Mercury was obtained on March 29, 2011. The probe finished a one-year mapping mission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MessCountdown_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-MessCountdown-204">[199]</a></sup> and then entered a one-year extended mission into 2013. In addition to continued observations and mapping of Mercury, <i>MESSENGER</i> observed the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">solar maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extended2013_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extended2013-206">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mission was designed to clear up six key issues: Mercury's high density, its geological history, the nature of its magnetic field, the structure of its core, whether it has ice at its poles, and where its tenuous atmosphere comes from. To this end, the probe carried imaging devices that gathered much-higher-resolution images of much more of Mercury than <span class="nowrap"><i>Mariner 10</i></span>, assorted <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometers</a> to determine abundances of elements in the crust, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometers</a> and devices to measure velocities of charged particles. Measurements of changes in the probe's orbital velocity were expected to be used to infer details of the planet's interior structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-messenger_faq_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-messenger_faq-207">[202]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> final maneuver was on April 24, 2015, and it crashed into Mercury's surface on April 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_2015_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-results_2015-208">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150427_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150427-209">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150430_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150430-210">[205]</a></sup> The spacecraft's impact with Mercury occurred near 3:26 pm EDT on April 30, 2015, leaving a crater estimated to be 16 m (52 ft) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="BepiColombo"><i>BepiColombo</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> and the <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">Japanese Space Agency</a> developed and launched a joint mission called <i>BepiColombo</i>, which will orbit Mercury with two probes: one to map the planet and the other to study its magnetosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAColumboGoAhead_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESAColumboGoAhead-212">[207]</a></sup> Launched on October 20, 2018, <i>BepiColombo</i> is expected to reach Mercury in 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[208]</a></sup> It will release a magnetometer probe into an elliptical orbit, then chemical rockets will fire to deposit the mapper probe into a circular orbit. Both probes will operate for one terrestrial year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAColumboGoAhead_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESAColumboGoAhead-212">[207]</a></sup> The mapper probe carries an array of spectrometers similar to those on <i>MESSENGER</i>, and will study the planet at many different wavelengths including <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[209]</a></sup> <i>BepiColombo</i> conducted the first of its six planned Mercury flybys on October 1, 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[210]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-rotation/revolution-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-rotation/revolution_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> In astronomy, the words "rotation" and "revolution" have different meanings. "Rotation" is the turning of a body about an axis that passes through the body, as in "Earth rotates once a day." "Revolution" is motion around a centre that is external to the body, usually in orbit, as in "Earth takes a year for each revolution around the Sun." The verbs "rotate" and "revolve" mean doing rotation and revolution, respectively.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-dwarf_planet-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-dwarf_planet_20-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> was considered a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from its discovery in 1930 to 2006, but after that it has been reclassified as a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>. Pluto's orbital eccentricity is greater than Mercury's. Pluto is also smaller than Mercury, but was thought to be larger until 1976.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-angular-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-angular_116-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The Sun's total angular displacement during its apparent retrograde motion as seen from the surface of Mercury is ~1.23°, while the Sun's angular diameter when the apparent retrograde motion begins and ends is ~1.71°, increasing to ~1.73° at perihelion (midway through the retrograde motion).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is important to be clear about the meaning of "closeness". In the astronomical literature, the term "closest planets" often means "the two planets that approach each other most closely". In other words, the orbits of the two planets approach each other most closely. However, this does not mean that the two planets are closest over time. For example, essentially because Mercury is closer to the Sun than Venus, Mercury spends more time in proximity to Earth; it could, therefore, be said that Mercury is the planet that is "closest to Earth when averaged over time". However, using this time-average definition of 'closeness'—as noted above—it turns out that Mercury is the closest planet to <i>all</i> other planets in the solar system. For that reason, arguably, the proximity-definition is not particularly helpful. An episode of the BBC Radio 4 programme "More or Less" explains the different notions of proximity well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoreOrLess_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoreOrLess-121">[118]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Cuneiform_MUL-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cuneiform_MUL_154-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Some sources precede the cuneiform transcription with "MUL". "MUL" is a cuneiform sign that was used in the Sumerian language to designate a star or planet, but it is not considered part of the actual name. The "4" is a reference number in the Sumero–Akkadian transliteration system to designate which of several syllables a certain cuneiform sign is most likely designating.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.greek-names.info/greek-names-of-the-planets/" rel="nofollow">"Greek Names of the Planets"</a>. April 25, 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 14,</span> 2012</span>. <q><i>Ermis</i> is the Greek name of the planet Mercury, which is the closest planet to the Sun. It is named after the Greek God of commerce, Ermis or Hermes, who was also the messenger of the Ancient Greek gods.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Greek+Names+of+the+Planets&amp;rft.date=2010-04-25&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.greek-names.info%2Fgreek-names-of-the-planets%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMercury+%28planet%29"></span> See also the <a class="extiw" href="https://el.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%95%CF%81%CE%BC%CE%AE%CF%82_(%CF%80%CE%BB%CE%B1%CE%BD%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82)" title="el:Ερμής (πλανήτης)">Greek article about the planet</a>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDuncan1946">Duncan, John Charles (1946). <i>Astronomy: A Textbook</i>. <li id="cite_note-jones-1999-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-jones-1999_30-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFJones1999">Jones, Alexander (1999). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8MokzymQ43IC" rel="nofollow"><i>Astronomical papyri from Oxyrhynchus</i></a>. pp. 62–63. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780871692337" title="Special:BookSources/9780871692337"><bdi>9780871692337</bdi></a>. <q>It is now possible to trace the medieval symbols for at least four of the five planets to forms that occur in some of the latest papyrus horoscopes ([ <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus_Papyri" title="Oxyrhynchus Papyri">P.Oxy.</a> ] 4272, 4274, 4275 [...]).
